					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Annual migration of salmon</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the annual salmon migration. For the video game, see <a href="/wiki/Salmon_Run_(video_game)" title="Salmon Run (video game)">Salmon Run (video game)</a>. For the mode in the Splatoon series, see <a href="/wiki/Splatoon_2" title="Splatoon 2">Splatoon 2</a> and <a href="/wiki/Splatoon_3" title="Splatoon 3">Splatoon 3</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Grizzly_Bear_Fishing_Brooks_Falls_(flipped).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Grizzly_Bear_Fishing_Brooks_Falls_%28flipped%29.jpg/250px-Grizzly_Bear_Fishing_Brooks_Falls_%28flipped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="375" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Grizzly_Bear_Fishing_Brooks_Falls_%28flipped%29.jpg/500px-Grizzly_Bear_Fishing_Brooks_Falls_%28flipped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1726" data-file-height="2592" /></a><figcaption>A <a href="/wiki/Grizzly_bear" title="Grizzly bear">grizzly bear</a> ambushing a jumping salmon during an annual salmon run</figcaption></figure>
<p>A <b>salmon run</b> is an annual <a href="/wiki/Fish_migration" title="Fish migration">fish migration</a> event where many <a href="/wiki/Salmonid" class="mw-redirect" title="Salmonid">salmonid</a> species, which are typically hatched in <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">fresh water</a> and live most of their adult life downstream in the <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">ocean</a>, swim back against the stream to the upper reaches of rivers to <a href="/wiki/Spawn_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Spawn (biology)">spawn</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Gravel" title="Gravel">gravel</a> beds of small <a href="/wiki/Stream" title="Stream">creeks</a>. After spawning, most <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_salmon" title="Atlantic salmon">Atlantic salmon</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and all species of <a href="/wiki/Oncorhynchus" title="Oncorhynchus">Pacific salmon</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> die, and the salmon <a href="/wiki/Biological_life_cycle" title="Biological life cycle">life cycle</a> starts over again with the new generation of <a href="/wiki/Hatchling" title="Hatchling">hatchlings</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">Salmon</a> are <a href="/wiki/Anadromous" class="mw-redirect" title="Anadromous">anadromous</a>, spending their <a href="/wiki/Juvenile_fish" title="Juvenile fish">juvenile</a> life in rivers or lakes, and then migrating out to sea where they spend <a href="/wiki/Adult" title="Adult">adult</a> lives and gain most of their <a href="/wiki/Body_mass" class="mw-redirect" title="Body mass">body mass</a>. When they reach <a href="/wiki/Sexual_maturity" title="Sexual maturity">sexual maturity</a>, the adults return to the upstream rivers to <a href="/wiki/Reproduce" class="mw-redirect" title="Reproduce">reproduce</a>. Usually they <a href="/wiki/Natal_homing" title="Natal homing">return with uncanny precision</a> to the natal river where they were born, and even to the very spawning ground of their birth. It is thought that, when they are in the ocean, they use <a href="/wiki/Magnetoreception" title="Magnetoreception">magnetoreception</a> to locate the general position of their natal river, and once close to the river, that they use their sense of smell to <a href="/wiki/Homing_(biology)" title="Homing (biology)">home in on</a> the river entrance and even their natal spawning ground.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Trout" title="Trout">Trout</a>, which are sister species of salmon, also perform similar migrations, although they mostly move <a href="/wiki/Potamodromous" class="mw-redirect" title="Potamodromous">potamodromously</a> between creeks and large freshwater lakes, except for some coastal/<a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuary</a> subspecies such as <a href="/wiki/Steelhead" title="Steelhead">steelhead</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sea_trout" title="Sea trout">sea trout</a> that migrate seasonally between <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">salty</a>/<a href="/wiki/Brackish" class="mw-redirect" title="Brackish">brackish</a> and fresh water just like salmon do. There are also landlocked populations of some salmon species that have adapted to spend their entire life in freshwater like trout.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest" title="Pacific Northwest">Northwest America</a>, salmons are <a href="/wiki/Keystone_species" title="Keystone species">keystone species</a>, which means the ecological impact they have on other wildlife is greater than would be expected in relation to their <a href="/wiki/Biomass_(ecology)" title="Biomass (ecology)">biomass</a>. Most salmon species migrate during the <a href="/wiki/Autumn" title="Autumn">autumn</a> (September through November),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which coincides with the pre-<a href="/wiki/Winter" title="Winter">winter</a> activities of many <a href="/wiki/Hibernating" class="mw-redirect" title="Hibernating">hibernating</a> animals. The annual salmon run can be a major feeding event for <a href="/wiki/Predator" class="mw-redirect" title="Predator">predators</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Grizzly_bears" class="mw-redirect" title="Grizzly bears">grizzly bears</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bald_eagle" title="Bald eagle">bald eagles</a>, as well as an important <a href="/wiki/Fishing_season" class="mw-redirect" title="Fishing season">window period</a> for <a href="/wiki/Recreational_fishing" title="Recreational fishing">sport fishermen</a>.
</p><p>The post-spawning death of salmon also has important ecological consequences, because the significant <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrients</a> in their <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">carcasses</a>, rich in <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, are transferred from the ocean and released to inland <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_ecosystem" title="Aquatic ecosystem">aquatic ecosystems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_animal" title="Terrestrial animal">terrestrial animals</a> (such as bears) and the <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetlands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Riparian" class="mw-redirect" title="Riparian">riparian</a> <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">woodlands</a> adjacent to the rivers. This has knock-on effects not only for the next generation of salmon, but to every wildlife species living in the riparian zones the salmon reach.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Helfield2006&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helfield2006-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The nutrients can also be washed downstream into <a href="/wiki/Estuaries" class="mw-redirect" title="Estuaries">estuaries</a> where they accumulate and provide significant support for <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> and estuarine-breeding <a href="/wiki/Waterbird" class="mw-redirect" title="Waterbird">waterbirds</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />

<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Juvenile_fish#Juvenile_salmon" title="Juvenile fish">Juvenile fish §&#160;Juvenile salmon</a></div>
<p>Most salmon are <a href="/wiki/Anadromous" class="mw-redirect" title="Anadromous">anadromous</a>, a term which comes from the Greek <i>anadromos</i>, meaning "running upward".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Webster&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Anadromous fish grow up mostly in the saltwater in oceans. When they have matured they <a href="/wiki/Fish_migration" title="Fish migration">migrate</a> or "run up" freshwater rivers to <a href="/wiki/Spawn_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Spawn (biology)">spawn</a> in what is called the salmon run.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Moyle188&#95;5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle188-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Anadromous salmon are Northern Hemisphere fish that spend their ocean phase in either the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>. They do not thrive in warm water. There is only one species of salmon found in the Atlantic, commonly called the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_salmon" title="Atlantic salmon">Atlantic salmon</a>. These salmon run up rivers on both sides of the ocean. Seven different species of salmon inhabit the Pacific (see table), and these are collectively referred to as <a href="/wiki/Pacific_salmon" class="mw-redirect" title="Pacific salmon">Pacific salmon</a>. Five of these species run up rivers on both sides of the Pacific, but two species are found only on the Asian side.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FAQ&#95;6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAQ-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the early 19th century, Chinook salmon were successfully established in the Southern Hemisphere, far from their native range, in New Zealand rivers. Attempts to establish anadromous salmon elsewhere have not succeeded.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Walrond&#95;7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walrond-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<p>The <a href="/wiki/Parr_(fish)" class="mw-redirect" title="Parr (fish)">life cycle</a> of an anadromous salmon begins and, if it survives the full course of its natural life, usually ends in a gravel bed in the upper reaches of a stream or river. These are the salmon <a href="/wiki/Spawning" title="Spawning">spawning</a> grounds where salmon <a href="/wiki/Fish_egg" class="mw-redirect" title="Fish egg">eggs</a> are deposited, for safety, in the gravel. The salmon spawning grounds are also the salmon nurseries, providing a more protected environment than the ocean usually offers. After 2 to 6 months the eggs hatch into tiny larvae called <i>sac fry</i> or <i>alevin</i>. The alevin have a sac containing the remainder of the yolk, and they stay hidden in the gravel while they feed on the yolk. When the yolk has gone they must find food for themselves, so they leave the protection of the gravel and start feeding on <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">plankton</a>. At this point the baby salmon are called <i>fry</i>. At the end of the summer the fry develop into juvenile fish called <i>parr</i>. Parr feed on small <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> and are camouflaged with a pattern of spots and vertical bars. They remain in this stage for up to three years.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bley1988&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bley1988-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Lindberg2011&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lindberg2011-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>As they approach the time when they are ready to migrate out to the sea the parr lose their camouflage bars and undergo a process of physiological changes which allows them to survive the shift from freshwater to saltwater. At this point salmon are called <i>smolt</i>. Smolt spend time in the brackish waters of the river estuary while their body chemistry adjusts their <a href="/wiki/Osmoregulation" title="Osmoregulation">osmoregulation</a> to cope with the higher salt levels they will encounter in the ocean.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-SalmonTrust&#95;18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SalmonTrust-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Smolt also grow the silvery scales which visually confuse ocean predators. When they have matured sufficiently in late spring, and are about 15 to 20 centimetres long, the smolt swim out of the rivers and into the sea. There they spend their first year as a <i>post-smolt</i>. Post-smolt form <a href="/wiki/Shoaling_and_schooling" title="Shoaling and schooling">schools</a> with other post-smolt, and set off to find deep-sea feeding grounds. They then spend up to four more years as adult ocean salmon while their full swimming ability and reproductive capacity develop.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bley1988&#95;16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bley1988-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Lindberg2011&#95;17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lindberg2011-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-SalmonTrust&#95;18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SalmonTrust-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Then, in one of the animal kingdom's more extreme <a href="/wiki/Animal_migration" title="Animal migration">migrations</a>, the salmon return from the saltwater ocean back to a freshwater river to spawn afresh.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Crossin2009&#95;19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crossin2009-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;" class=""></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Return_from_the_ocean">Return from the ocean</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salmon_run&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Return from the ocean"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Salmon_jumping.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ec/Salmon_jumping.jpg" decoding="async" width="166" height="250" class="mw-file-element" data-file-width="166" data-file-height="250" /></a><figcaption><div class="center">Salmon jumping a fall</div></figcaption></figure>
<p>After several years wandering huge distances in the ocean, most salmon return to the natal rivers where they were spawned. Then most of them swim up the rivers until they reach the very spawning ground that was their original birthplace.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Moyle190&#95;20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle190-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There are various theories about how this happens. One theory is that there are geomagnetic and chemical cues which the salmon use to guide them back to their birthplace. The fish may be sensitive to the Earth's magnetic field, which could allow the fish to orient itself in the ocean, so it can navigate back to the <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuary</a> of its natal stream.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Lohmann2008&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lohmann2008-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Salmon have a strong sense of smell. Speculation about whether odours provide homing cues go back to the 19th century.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Trevanius&#95;22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trevanius-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1951, Hasler hypothesised that, once in vicinity of the <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuary</a> or entrance to its birth river, salmon may use chemical cues which they can smell, and which are unique to their natal stream, as a mechanism to <a href="/wiki/Homing_(biology)" title="Homing (biology)">home onto</a> the entrance of the stream.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hasler1951&#95;23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hasler1951-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1978, Hasler and his students found that the way salmon locate their home rivers with such precision was because they could recognise its characteristic smell. They further demonstrated that the smell of their river becomes imprinted in salmon when they transform into smolts, just before they migrate out to sea.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Moyle190&#95;20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle190-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hasler1978&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hasler1978-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dittman&#95;25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dittman-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Homecoming salmon can also recognise characteristic smells in tributary streams as they move up the main river. They may also be sensitive to characteristic <a href="/wiki/Pheromone" title="Pheromone">pheromones</a> given off by juvenile <a href="/wiki/Conspecific" class="mw-redirect" title="Conspecific">conspecifics</a>. There is evidence that they can "discriminate between two populations of their own species".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Moyle190&#95;20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle190-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Groot1986&#95;26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Groot1986-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The recognition that each river and tributary has its own characteristic smell, and the role this plays as a navigation aid, led to a widespread search for a mechanism or mechanisms that might allow salmon to navigate over long distances in the open ocean. In 1977, Leggett identified, as mechanisms worth investigating, the use of the sun for navigation, and orientation to various possible gradients, such as temperature, salinity or chemicals gradients, or geomagnetic or geoelectric fields.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Leggett&#95;27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leggett-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Moyle191&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle191-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There is little evidence salmon use clues from the sun for navigation. Migrating salmon have been observed maintaining direction at nighttime and when it is cloudy. Likewise, electronically tagged salmon were observed to maintain direction even when swimming in water much too deep for sunlight to be of use.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ogura&#95;29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ogura-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 1973, it was shown that Atlantic salmon have conditioned cardiac responses to electric fields with strengths similar to those found in oceans. "This sensitivity might allow a migrating fish to align itself upstream or downstream in an ocean current in the absence of fixed references."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rommel&#95;30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rommel-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1988, researchers found iron, in the form of <a href="/wiki/Single_domain_(magnetic)" title="Single domain (magnetic)">single domain</a> <a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">magnetite</a>, resides in the skulls of sockeye salmon. The quantities present are sufficient for <a href="/wiki/Magnetoception" class="mw-redirect" title="Magnetoception">magnetoception</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Quinn1988&#95;31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quinn1988-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Tagging studies have shown a small number of fish do not find their natal rivers, but travel instead up other, usually nearby streams or rivers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Quinn1991&#95;32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quinn1991-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Tallman&#95;33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tallman-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is important some salmon stray from their home areas; otherwise new habitats could not be colonized. In 1984, Quinn hypothesized there is a dynamic equilibrium, controlled by genes, between homing and straying.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Quinn1984&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quinn1984-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> If the spawning grounds have a uniform high quality, then natural selection should favour the descendants that home accurately. If the spawning grounds have a variable quality, then natural selection should favour a mixture of the descendants that stray and the descendants that home accurately.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Lohmann2008&#95;21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lohmann2008-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Quinn1984&#95;34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quinn1984-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:The_kype_of_a_spawning_male_salmon.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1a/The_kype_of_a_spawning_male_salmon.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="143" class="mw-file-element" data-file-width="240" data-file-height="180" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Kype" title="Kype">kype</a> of a spawning male salmon</figcaption></figure>
<p>Prior to the run up the river, the salmon undergo profound physiological changes. <a href="/wiki/Undulatory_locomotion#Muscle_architecture" title="Undulatory locomotion">Fish swim</a> by contracting longitudinal red muscle and obliquely oriented white muscles. Red muscles are used for sustained activity, such as ocean migrations. White muscles are used for bursts of activity, such as bursts of speed or jumping.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kapoor&#95;35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kapoor-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As the salmon comes to end of its ocean migration and enters the estuary of its natal river, its energy metabolism is faced with two major challenges: it must supply energy suitable for swimming the river rapids, and it must supply the sperm and eggs required for the reproductive events ahead. The water in the estuary receives the freshwater discharge from the natal river. Relative to ocean water, this has a high chemical load from <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">surface runoff</a>. Researchers in 2009 found evidence that, as the salmon encounter the resulting drop in salinity and increase in olfactory stimulation, two key metabolic changes are triggered: there is a switch from using red muscles for swimming to using white muscles, and there is an increase in the sperm and egg load. "Pheromones at the spawning grounds [trigger] a second shift to further enhance reproductive loading."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Miller&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The salmon also undergo radical morphological changes as they prepare for the spawning event ahead. All salmon lose the silvery blue they had as ocean fish, and their colour darkens, sometimes with a radical change in hue. Salmon are <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexually dimorphic</a>, and the male salmon develop canine-like teeth and their jaws develop a pronounced curve or hook (<i><a href="/wiki/Kype" title="Kype">kype</a></i>). Some species of male salmon grow large humps.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DFWa&#95;37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DFWa-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Obstacles">Obstacles</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salmon_run&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Obstacles"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1273380762/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" /><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:328px;max-width:328px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:207px;max-width:207px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Fish_ladder_at_Tawe_Barrage_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1607929.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Fish_ladder_at_Tawe_Barrage_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1607929.jpg/250px-Fish_ladder_at_Tawe_Barrage_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1607929.jpg" decoding="async" width="205" height="154" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Fish_ladder_at_Tawe_Barrage_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1607929.jpg/500px-Fish_ladder_at_Tawe_Barrage_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1607929.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">A fish ladder makes it easier for salmon to negotiate a <a href="/wiki/Weir" title="Weir">weir</a>.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:117px;max-width:117px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:RockyReachDam-FishLadder.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/RockyReachDam-FishLadder.jpg/120px-RockyReachDam-FishLadder.jpg" decoding="async" width="115" height="153" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/RockyReachDam-FishLadder.jpg/250px-RockyReachDam-FishLadder.jpg 2x" data-file-width="772" data-file-height="1024" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">An extended bypass</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Salmon start the run in peak condition, the culmination of years of development in the ocean. They need high swimming and leaping abilities to battle the rapids and other obstacles the river may present, and they need a full sexual development to ensure a successful spawn at the end of the run. All their energy goes into the physical rigours of the journey and the dramatic morphological transformations they must still complete before they are ready for the spawning events ahead.
</p><p>The run up the river can be exhausting, sometimes requiring the salmon to battle hundreds of miles upstream against strong currents and rapids. They cease feeding during the run.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Moyle188&#95;5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle188-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Chinook and sockeye salmon from central Idaho must travel 900 miles (1,400&#160;km) and climb nearly 7,000 feet (2,100&#160;m) before they are ready to spawn. Salmon deaths that occur on the upriver journey are referred to as <i>en route mortality</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jeffries2011&#95;38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffries2011-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Salmon negotiate <a href="/wiki/Waterfall" title="Waterfall">waterfalls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rapids" title="Rapids">rapids</a> by leaping or jumping. They have been recorded making vertical jumps as high as 3.65 metres (12&#160;ft).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Beach1984&#95;39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beach1984-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The height that can be achieved by a salmon depends on the position of the <a href="/wiki/Standing_wave" title="Standing wave">standing wave</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_jump" title="Hydraulic jump">hydraulic jump</a> at the base of the fall, as well as how deep the water is.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Beach1984&#95;39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beach1984-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fish_ladder" title="Fish ladder">Fish ladders</a>, or fishways, are specially designed to help salmon and other fish to bypass dams and other man-made obstructions, and continue on to their spawning grounds further upriver.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-MichiganDNR&#95;40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MichiganDNR-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Data suggest that navigation locks have a potential to be operated as vertical slot fishways to provide increased access for a range of biota, including poor swimmers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (March 2018)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;" class=""></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1273380762/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" /><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:328px;max-width:328px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:212px;max-width:212px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Black_Bear_Caught_A_Salmon.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Black_Bear_Caught_A_Salmon.jpg/250px-Black_Bear_Caught_A_Salmon.jpg" decoding="async" width="210" height="166" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Black_Bear_Caught_A_Salmon.jpg/500px-Black_Bear_Caught_A_Salmon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="599" data-file-height="473" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">The black fur of <a href="/wiki/American_black_bear" title="American black bear">black bears</a> is easily spotted by salmon in daylight, and the bears fish more successfully using auditory clues at night.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:112px;max-width:112px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Spiritbear.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Spiritbear.jpg/120px-Spiritbear.jpg" decoding="async" width="110" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Spiritbear.jpg/250px-Spiritbear.jpg 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="451" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">White-coated <a href="/wiki/Kermode_bear" class="mw-redirect" title="Kermode bear">Kermode bears</a> have more success fishing in daylight.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Skilled predators, such as <a href="/wiki/Bear" title="Bear">bears</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bald_eagle" title="Bald eagle">bald eagles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Recreational_fishing" title="Recreational fishing">fishermen</a> can await the salmon during the run. Normally solitary animals, <a href="/wiki/Grizzly_bear" title="Grizzly bear">grizzly bears</a> congregate by streams and rivers when the salmon spawn.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Helfield2006&#95;3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helfield2006-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hilderbrand&#95;42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hilderbrand-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Predation from <a href="/wiki/Harbor_seals" class="mw-redirect" title="Harbor seals">harbor seals</a>, <a href="/wiki/California_sea_lions" class="mw-redirect" title="California sea lions">California sea lions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Steller_sea_lions" class="mw-redirect" title="Steller sea lions">Steller sea lions</a> can pose a significant threat, even in river ecosystems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/American_black_bear" title="American black bear">Black bears</a> also fish the salmon. Black bears usually operate during the day, but when it comes to salmon they tend to fish at night.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Klinka2009a&#95;45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klinka2009a-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This is partly to avoid competition with the more powerful brown bears, but it is also because they catch more salmon at night.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Reimchen2009&#95;46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reimchen2009-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During the day, salmon are very evasive and attuned to visual clues, but at night they focus on their spawning activities, generating acoustic clues the bears tune into.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Klinka2009a&#95;45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klinka2009a-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Black bears may also fish for salmon during the night because their black fur is easily spotted by salmon in the daytime. In 2009, researchers compared the foraging success of black bears with the white-coated <a href="/wiki/Kermode_bear" class="mw-redirect" title="Kermode bear">Kermode bear</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(biology)" title="Polymorphism (biology)">morphed</a> subspecies of the black bear. They found the Kermode bear had no more success catching salmon at night time, but had greater success than the black bears during the day.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Klinka2009b&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klinka2009b-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Otter" title="Otter">Otters</a> are also common predators. In 2011, researchers showed that when otters predate salmon, the salmon can "sniff them out". They demonstrated that once otters have eaten salmon, the remaining salmon could detect and avoid the waters where otter faeces was present.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Roberts&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-PlanetEarth&#95;49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PlanetEarth-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;" class=""></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Spawning">Spawning</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salmon_run&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Spawning"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Juvenile_salmon" class="mw-redirect" title="Juvenile salmon">Juvenile salmon</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1273380762/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" /><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px"><div class="trow"><div class="theader" style="text-align:center">Salmon redds</div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Becharof_Wilderness_Salmon.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Becharof_Wilderness_Salmon.jpg/330px-Becharof_Wilderness_Salmon.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="198" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Becharof_Wilderness_Salmon.jpg/960px-Becharof_Wilderness_Salmon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1011" data-file-height="668" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Spawning salmon building redds on a riffle</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Salmon_redds.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Salmon_redds.jpg/330px-Salmon_redds.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="204" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Salmon_redds.jpg/960px-Salmon_redds.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3894" data-file-height="2652" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">The white areas on the river bottom are completed redds.</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Redds.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Redds.jpg/330px-Redds.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="225" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Redds.jpg/960px-Redds.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4032" data-file-height="3024" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Closeup of redds on a riverbed</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The term <i>prespawn mortality</i> is used to refer to fish that arrive successfully at the spawning grounds, and then die without spawning. Prespawn mortality is surprisingly variable, with one study observing rates between 3% and 90%.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jeffries2011&#95;38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffries2011-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gilhousen1990&#95;50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gilhousen1990-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Factors that contribute to these mortalities include high temperatures,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Crossin2008&#95;51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crossin2008-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Farrell&#95;52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Farrell-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> high river discharge rates,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rand&#95;53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rand-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and parasites and diseases.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gilhousen1990&#95;50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gilhousen1990-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jones&#95;54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> "At present there are no reliable indicators to predict whether an individual arriving at a spawning area will in fact survive to spawn."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jeffries2011&#95;38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffries2011-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The eggs of a female salmon are called her <a href="/wiki/Roe" title="Roe">roe</a>. To lay her roe, the female salmon builds a spawning nest, called a <i>redd</i>, in a riffle with gravel as its <a href="/wiki/Streambed" class="mw-redirect" title="Streambed">streambed</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Riffle" title="Riffle">riffle</a> is a relatively shallow length of stream where the water is turbulent and flows faster. She builds the redd by using her tail (<a href="/wiki/Caudal_fin" class="mw-redirect" title="Caudal fin">caudal fin</a>) to create a low-pressure zone, lifting gravel to be swept downstream, and excavating a shallow depression.  The redd may contain up to 5,000 eggs, each about the size of a pea, covering 30 square feet (2.8&#160;m<sup>2</sup>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-McGrath&#95;55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGrath-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The eggs usually range from orange to red. One or more males will approach the female in her redd, depositing his sperm, or milt, over her eggs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-USFWS&#95;56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USFWS-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The female then covers the eggs by disturbing the gravel at the upstream edge of the depression before moving on to make another redd. The female will make as many as seven redds before her supply of eggs is exhausted.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-USFWS&#95;56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USFWS-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-DFWb&#95;57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DFWb-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Male pink salmon and some sockeye salmon develop pronounced humps just before they spawn. These humps may have evolved because they confer species advantages. The humps make it less likely the salmon will spawn in the shallow water at margins of the streambed, which tend to dry out during low water flows or freeze in winter. Further, riffles can contain many salmon spawning simultaneously, as in the image on the right. Predators, such as bears, will be more likely to catch the more visually prominent humped males, with their humps projecting above the surface of the water. This may provide a protective buffer for the females.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Groot1991&#95;58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Groot1991-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Dominant male salmon defend their redds by rushing at and chasing intruders. They butt and bite them with the canine-like teeth they developed for the spawning event. The kypes are used to clamp around the base of the tail (<a href="/wiki/Caudal_peduncle" class="mw-redirect" title="Caudal peduncle">caudal peduncle</a>) of an opponent.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Groot1991&#95;58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Groot1991-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Deterioration">Deterioration</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salmon_run&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Deterioration"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The physical condition of the salmon deteriorates the longer they remain in fresh water. Once the salmon have spawned, most of them deteriorate rapidly (a.k.a. "spawned out") and soon die. Some deteriorating salmon are still alive, but their bodies have already begun the process of rotting,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and these deteriorating salmon are sometimes colloquially called "zombie fish".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This is because upstream freshwater bodies (especially creeks) typically do not have sufficient food available for the adult salmon diet, and they have used large amounts of energy swimming upriver, thus exhausting their own internal nutrient reserves.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Spawning salmon also have programmed <a href="/wiki/Senescence" title="Senescence">senescence</a>, which is "characterized by <a href="/wiki/Immunosuppression" title="Immunosuppression">immunosuppression</a> and organ deterioration", making them more vulnerable to diseases.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jeffries2011&#95;38-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffries2011-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dickhoff&#95;63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dickhoff-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Finch&#95;64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Finch-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Most zombie fish die within days of spawning, but some can last up to a couple of weeks.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Once the salmon die in the river, they are either <a href="/wiki/Scavenge" class="mw-redirect" title="Scavenge">scavenged</a> by other animals, or they <a href="/wiki/Decompose" class="mw-redirect" title="Decompose">decompose</a> and release inorganic nutrients to the <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">plankton</a> in the river and the <a href="/wiki/Riparian" class="mw-redirect" title="Riparian">riparian</a> vegetation in the <a href="/wiki/Floodplain" title="Floodplain">floodplain</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Pacific_salmon" class="mw-redirect" title="Pacific salmon">Pacific salmon</a> are a classic example of a <a href="/wiki/Semelparous" class="mw-redirect" title="Semelparous">semelparous</a> animal, which reproduce only once in their lifetime. Semelparity is sometimes called "big bang" reproduction, since the single reproductive event of semelparous organisms is usually large and fatal to the spawners.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ricklefs&#95;65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ricklefs-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is an evolutionary strategy that concentrates all available resources into maximizing reproduction, at the expense of individual organism's life, which is common among <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> but rare among <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> All six species of Pacific salmons live for many years in the ocean before swimming to the freshwater stream of its birth, spawning, and then dying. Most <a href="/wiki/Atlantic" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlantic">Atlantic</a> salmon also die after spawning, but about 5 to 10% (mostly female) return to the ocean where they can recover and spawn again the next season.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-SalmonTrust&#95;18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SalmonTrust-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1273380762/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" /><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" data-file-height="1006" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Spawning male <a href="/wiki/Sockeye_salmon" title="Sockeye salmon">sockeye salmon</a></div></div><div src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Salmo_salar_eggs.jpg/250px-Salmo_salar_eggs.jpg" decoding="async" width="130" height="136" class="mw-file-element" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">All Pacific salmon <i>(pictured)</i> and most Atlantic salmon die after spawning.</div></div></div></div></div>
<div style="clear:both;" class=""></div>
<div style="clear:both;" class=""></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Keystone_species">Keystone species</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salmon_run&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Keystone species"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1273380762/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" /><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Brown_bears_with_salmon_carcass.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Brown_bears_with_salmon_carcass.jpg/250px-Brown_bears_with_salmon_carcass.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="177" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f3/Brown_bears_with_salmon_carcass.jpg 2x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="281" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Grizzly bears tend to carry salmon carcasses into adjacent riparian areas.</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Salmon_nitrogen_cycling_in_a_stream.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Salmon_nitrogen_cycling_in_a_stream.gif/250px-Salmon_nitrogen_cycling_in_a_stream.gif" decoding="async" width="220" height="155" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Salmon_nitrogen_cycling_in_a_stream.gif/500px-Salmon_nitrogen_cycling_in_a_stream.gif 2x" data-file-width="624" data-file-height="441" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Salmon subsidy to the <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_cycle" title="Nitrogen cycle">nitrogen cycle</a> in a hypothetical stream system</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>In the Pacific Northwest and Alaska, salmon is a <a href="/wiki/Keystone_species" title="Keystone species">keystone species</a>, supporting wildlife from birds to bears and otters.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Willson&#95;66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willson-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  The bodies of salmon represent a transfer of nutrients from the ocean, rich in nitrogen, sulfur, carbon and phosphorus, to the <a href="/wiki/Forest_ecosystem" class="mw-redirect" title="Forest ecosystem">forest ecosystem</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Grizzly_bear" title="Grizzly bear">Grizzly bears</a> function as <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_engineer" title="Ecosystem engineer">ecosystem engineers</a>, capturing salmon and carrying them into adjacent wooded areas. There they deposit nutrient-rich urine and feces and partially eaten carcasses. It has been estimated that bears leave up to half the salmon they harvest on the forest floor,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Reimchen2001&#95;67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reimchen2001-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Quinn2009&#95;68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quinn2009-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in densities that can reach 4,000 kilograms per hectare,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Reimchen2002&#95;69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reimchen2002-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> providing as much as 24% of the total nitrogen available to the riparian woodlands.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Helfield2006&#95;3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helfield2006-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The foliage of <a href="/wiki/Picea_glauca" title="Picea glauca">spruce trees</a> up to 500&#160;m (1,600&#160;ft) from a stream where grizzlies fish salmon have been found to contain nitrogen originating from fished salmon.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Helfield2006&#95;3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helfield2006-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1224211176">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:25%; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>Salmon continue to surprise us, showing us new ways in which their oceanic migrations eventually permeate entire terrestrial ecosystems. In terms of providing food and nutrients to a whole food web, we like to think of them as North America's answer to the Serengeti's <a href="/wiki/Wildebeest" title="Wildebeest">wildebeest</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ScienceDaily2008&#95;70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceDaily2008-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
</div>
<p>Wolves normally hunt for deer. A 2008 study suggests that when the salmon run starts, wolves choose to fish for salmon, even if plenty of deer are still available.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Darimont&#95;71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Darimont-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> "Selecting benign prey such as salmon makes sense from a safety point of view. While hunting deer, wolves commonly incur serious and often fatal injuries. In addition to safety benefits we determined that salmon also provides enhanced nutrition in terms of fat and energy."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ScienceDaily2008&#95;70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceDaily2008-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The upper reaches of the <a href="/wiki/Chilkat_River" title="Chilkat River">Chilkat River</a> in Alaska has particularly good spawning grounds. Each year these attract a run of up to half a million <a href="/wiki/Chum_salmon" title="Chum salmon">chum salmon</a>. As the salmon run up the river, <a href="/wiki/Bald_eagle" title="Bald eagle">bald eagles</a> arrive in their thousands to feast at the spawning grounds. This results in some of the world's largest congregations of bald eagles. The number of participating eagles is directly correlated with the number of spawning salmon.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hansen2008&#95;72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen2008-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Residual nutrients from salmon can also accumulate downstream in estuaries. A 2010 study suggests the density and diversity of many estuarine breeding birds in the summer "were strongly predicted by salmon <a href="/wiki/Biomass_(ecology)" title="Biomass (ecology)">biomass</a> in the autumn".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Field&#95;73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Field-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Anadromous salmon provide nutrients to these "diverse assemblages ... ecologically comparable to the migrating herds of wildebeest in the <a href="/wiki/Serengeti" title="Serengeti">Serengeti</a>".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Reimchen2002&#95;69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reimchen2002-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Prospects">Prospects</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salmon_run&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Prospects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In 1997, researchers noted that the future of salmon runs worldwide would depend on many factors, most of which are driven by human actions. Among the key driving factors are (1) harvest of salmon by commercial, recreational, and subsistence fishing, (2) alterations in stream and river channels, including construction of dikes and other riparian corridor modifications, (3) electricity generation, flood control, and irrigation supplied by dams, (4) alteration by humans of freshwater, <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuarine</a>, and marine environments used by salmon, coupled with aquatic changes due to climate and ocean circulatory regimes, (5) water withdrawals from rivers and reservoirs for agricultural, municipal, or commercial purposes,  (6) changes in climate caused at least in part by human activities, (7) competition from non-native fishes, (8) salmon predation by marine mammals, birds, and other fish species, (9) diseases and parasites, including those from outside the native region, and (10) reduced nutrient replenishment from decomposing salmon.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2009, <a href="/wiki/NOAA" class="mw-redirect" title="NOAA">NOAA</a> advised that continued runoff into North American rivers of three widely used <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> containing <a href="/wiki/Neurotoxin" title="Neurotoxin">neurotoxins</a> would "jeopardize the continued existence" of endangered and threatened Pacific salmon.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NOAA2009&#95;75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA2009-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-ENS&#95;76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ENS-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Global warming could see the end of some salmon runs by the end of the century,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (August 2023)">according to whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> such as the Californian runs of Chinook salmon.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Thompson&#95;77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-ScienceDaily2011&#95;78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceDaily2011-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A 2010 United Nations report said that increases in <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">acidification of oceans</a> would mean that shellfish such as <a href="/wiki/Pteropod" class="mw-redirect" title="Pteropod">Pteropods</a>, an important component of the ocean salmon diet, would be finding it more difficult to build their <a href="/wiki/Aragonite" title="Aragonite">aragonite</a> shells.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-UNEP&#95;79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNEP-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There were concerns<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (August 2023)">by whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> that this too could endanger future salmon runs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-TheTelegraph&#95;80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TheTelegraph-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salmon_run&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: In popular culture"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In a 1982 video game called <i><a href="/wiki/Salmon_Run_(video_game)" title="Salmon Run (video game)">Salmon Run</a></i>, the player takes the role of Sam the Salmon, swimming upriver to mate. Along the way he encounters waterfalls, a bear, fishermen, and seagulls.
</p><p>In the 2003 <a href="/wiki/The_Walt_Disney_Company" title="The Walt Disney Company">Disney</a> animated feature film, <i><a href="/wiki/Brother_Bear" title="Brother Bear">Brother Bear</a></i>, the protagonist Kenai adjusts to his life as a bear during the annual salmon run.
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Splatoon" title="Splatoon">Splatoon 2 and 3</a></i> introduces the game mode "<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://splatoonwiki.org/wiki/Salmon_Run">Salmon Run</a>" where players fight off fictional <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://splatoonwiki.org/wiki/Salmonid">Salmonids</a>, a race of salmon-like beings. Grizzco Industries, owned by <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://splatoonwiki.org/wiki/Mr._Grizz">Mr. Grizz</a>, employs players to hunt for salmonids and their eggs, similar to <a href="/wiki/Grizzly_bear" title="Grizzly bear">Grizzly bears</a> hunting salmon in nature. The game's lore suggests that the Salmonids participate in a massive salmon migration every 70 years, which coincides with the time the Splatoon series takes place.
</p><p>The 2023 eco fantasy film <i><a href="/wiki/Wild_Summon" title="Wild Summon">Wild Summon</a></i> depicts salmon as miniature aquatic humanlike free divers throughout their life cycle and migrations.
</p>

<dl><dt>Resilience</dt></dl>
<ul><li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFBottomJonesSimenstadSmith2009" class="citation journal cs1">Bottom DL, Jones KK, Simenstad CA, Smith CL (2009). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://dlc.dlib.indiana.edu/dlc/bitstream/handle/10535/2644/Reconnecting_Social_and_Ecological_Resilience_in_Salmon_Ecosystems.pdf?sequence=1&amp;isAllowed=y">"Reconnecting social and ecological resilience in salmon ecosystems"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i>Ecology and Society</i>. <b>14</b> (1) art5: 5. <a href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<span class="id-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.5751%2Fes-02734-140105">10.5751/es-02734-140105</a></span>.</cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Ecology+and+Society&amp;rft.atitle=Reconnecting+social+and+ecological+resilience+in+salmon+ecosystems&amp;rft.volume=14&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.artnum=art5&amp;rft.pages=5&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft&#95;id=info%3Adoi%2F10.5751%2Fes-02734-140105&amp;rft.aulast=Bottom&amp;rft.aufirst=DL&amp;rft.au=Jones%2C+KK&amp;rft.au=Simenstad%2C+CA&amp;rft.au=Smith%2C+CL&amp;rft&#95;id=http%3A%2F%2Fdlc.dlib.indiana.edu%2Fdlc%2Fbitstream%2Fhandle%2F10535%2F2644%2FReconnecting&#95;Social&#95;and&#95;Ecological&#95;Resilience&#95;in&#95;Salmon&#95;Ecosystems.pdf%3Fsequence%3D1%26isAllowed%3Dy&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASalmon+run" class="Z3988"></span></li>
